915 <section id="_558">

916

917 <h2>

918Who Is at Risk?</h2>

919

920 <div id="_section_558" class="pdq-sections">

921<div class="pdq-hp-patient-toggle">

922<a href="/types/breast/patient/breast-prevention-pdq">

923Go to Patient Version</a>

924</div>

925<p id="_559" tabindex="-1">

926Besides female sex, advancing age is the biggest risk factor for breast cancer. Reproductive factors that increase exposure to endogenous estrogen, such as early menarche and late menopause, increase risk, as does the use of combination estrogen-progesterone hormones after menopause. Nulliparity and alcohol consumption also are associated with increased risk.</p>

927<p id="_680" tabindex="-1">

928Women with a family history or personal history of invasive breast cancer, ductal carcinoma <em>

929in situ</em>

930 or lobular carcinoma <em>

931in situ</em>

932, or a history of breast biopsies that show benign proliferative disease have an increased risk of breast cancer.[<a href="#cit/section_1.1">

9331</a>

934-<a href="#cit/section_1.4">

9354</a>

936]</p>

937<p id="_560" tabindex="-1">

938Increased breast density is associated with increased risk. It is often a heritable trait but is also seen more frequently in nulliparous women, women whose first pregnancy occurs late in life, and women who use postmenopausal hormones and alcohol.</p>

939<p id="_561" tabindex="-1">

940Exposure to ionizing radiation, especially during puberty or young adulthood, and the inheritance of detrimental genetic mutations increase breast cancer risk.</p>

941<h6 do-not-show="toc">

942References</h6>

943<ol>

944<li id="section_1.1">

945Kotsopoulos J, Chen WY, Gates MA, et al.: Risk factors for ductal and lobular breast cancer: results from the nurses' health study. Breast Cancer Res 12 (6): R106, 2010.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=21143857&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=21143857&amp;dopt=Abstract">

946[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

947</li>

948<li id="section_1.2">

J Public Health (Oxf) 32 (4): 565-71, 2010.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=20154063&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=20154063&amp;dopt=Abstract">

958[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

1108<section id="_175">

1109<h3 id="_175_toc">

1110Factors With Adequate Evidence of Increased Risk of Breast Cancer</h3>

1111<section id="_562">

1112<h4 id="_562_toc">

1113Sex and age</h4>

1114<p id="_563" tabindex="-1">

1115Based on solid evidence, female sex and increasing age are the major risk factors for the development of breast cancer.</p>

1116<p id="_564" tabindex="-1">

1117<strong>

1118Magnitude of Effect</strong>

1119: Women have a lifetime risk of developing breast cancer that is approximately 100 times the risk for men. The short-term risk of breast cancer in a 70-year-old woman is about ten times that of a 30-year-old woman.</p>

1120<div class="pdq-content-list">

1121<ul id="_565" class="pdq-address-block">

1122<li>

1123<strong>

1124Study Design</strong>

1125: Many epidemiological trials.</li>

1126<li>

1127<strong>

1128Internal Validity</strong>

1129: Good.</li>

1130<li>

1131<strong>

1132Consistency</strong>

1133: Good.</li>

1134<li>

1135<strong>

1136External Validity</strong>

1137: Good.</li>

1138</ul>

1139</div>

1140</section>

1141<section id="_697">

1142<h4 id="_697_toc">

1143Inherited risk</h4>

1144<p id="_698" tabindex="-1">

1145Based on solid evidence, women who have a family history of breast cancer, especially in a first-degree relative, have an increased risk of breast cancer.</p>

1146<p id="_699" tabindex="-1">

1147<strong>

1148Magnitude of Effect</strong>

1149: Risk is doubled if a single first-degree relative is affected; risk is increased fivefold if two first-degree relatives are diagnosed.</p>

1150<div class="pdq-content-list">

1151<ul id="_700" class="pdq-address-block">

1152<li>

1153<strong>

1154Study Design</strong>

1155: Population studies, cohort studies, and case-control studies.</li>

1156<li>

1157<strong>

1158Internal Validity</strong>

1159: Good.</li>

1160<li>

1161<strong>

1162Consistency</strong>

1163: Good.</li>

1164<li>

1165<strong>

1166External Validity</strong>

1167: Good.</li>

1168</ul>

1169</div>

1170<p id="_567" tabindex="-1">

1171Based on solid evidence, women who inherit gene mutations associated with breast cancer have an increased risk.</p>

1172<p id="_568" tabindex="-1">

1173<strong>

1174Magnitude of Effect</strong>

1175: Variable, depending on gene mutation, family history, and other risk factors affecting gene expression.</p>

1176<div class="pdq-content-list">

1177<ul id="_569" class="pdq-address-block">

1178<li>

1179<strong>

1180Study Design</strong>

1181: Cohort or case-control studies.</li>

1182<li>

1183<strong>

1184Internal Validity</strong>

1185: Good.</li>

1186<li>

1187<strong>

1188Consistency</strong>

1189: Good.</li>

1190<li>

1191<strong>

1192External Validity</strong>

1193: Good.</li>

1194</ul>

1195</div>

1196</section>

1197<section id="_570">

1198<h4 id="_570_toc">

1199Breast density</h4>

1200<p id="_571" tabindex="-1">

1201Based on solid evidence, women with dense breasts have an increased risk of breast cancer. This is most often an inherent characteristic, to some extent modifiable by reproductive behavior, medications, and alcohol.[<a href="#cit/section_2.1">

12021</a>

1203]</p>

1204<p id="_572" tabindex="-1">

1205<strong>

1206Magnitude of Effect</strong>

1207: Women with dense breasts have increased risk, proportionate to the degree of density. This increased relative risk (RR) ranges from 1.79 for women with slightly increased density to 4.64 for women with very dense breasts, compared with women who have the lowest breast density.[<a href="#cit/section_2.2">

12082</a>

1209]</p>

1210<div class="pdq-content-list">

1211<ul id="_573" class="pdq-address-block">

1212<li>

1213<strong>

1214Study Design</strong>

1215: Cohort, case-control studies.</li>

1216<li>

1217<strong>

1218Internal Validity</strong>

1219: Good.</li>

1220<li>

1221<strong>

1222Consistency</strong>

1223: Good.</li>

1224<li>

1225<strong>

1226External Validity</strong>

1227: Good.</li>

1228</ul>

1229</div>

1230</section>

1231</section>

1232<section id="_574">

1233<h3 id="_574_toc">

1234Modifiable Factors With Adequate Evidence of Increased Risk of Breast Cancer</h3>

1235<section id="_793">

1236<h4 id="_793_toc">

1237Menopausal hormone therapy (MHT)</h4>

1238<p id="_794" tabindex="-1">

1239Based on solid evidence, MHT is associated with an increased risk of developing breast cancer, especially hormone-sensitive cancers. Estrogen-progesterone use significantly increases breast cancer risk starting with 1 to 4 years of usage and increases with duration of use. For estrogen use alone, the breast cancer risk is less but also significant. The excess risk persists after cessation of MHT.</p>

1240<div class="pdq-content-list">

1241<ul id="_795" class="pdq-address-block">

1242<li>

1243<strong>

1244Study Design</strong>

1245: Randomized controlled trials (RCTs), prospective studies and ecological observations.</li>

1246<li>

1247<strong>

1248Internal Validity</strong>

1249: Good.</li>

1250<li>

1251<strong>

1252Consistency</strong>

1253: Good.</li>

1254<li>

1255<strong>

1256External Validity</strong>

1257: Good.</li>

1258</ul>

1259</div>

1260</section>

1261<section id="_575">

1262<h4 id="_575_toc">

1263Combination hormone therapy</h4>

1264<p id="_576" tabindex="-1">

1265Based on solid evidence, combination hormone therapy (estrogen-progestin) is associated with an increased risk of developing breast cancer.</p>

1266<p id="_577" tabindex="-1">

1267<strong>

1268Magnitude of Effect</strong>

1269: Approximately a 26% increase in incidence of invasive breast cancer; the number needed to produce one excess breast cancer is 237.</p>

1270<div class="pdq-content-list">

1271<ul id="_578" class="pdq-address-block">

1272<li>

1273<strong>

1274Study Design</strong>

1275: RCTs and ecological observations. Furthermore, cohort and ecological studies show that cessation of combination HT is associated with a decrease in rates of breast cancer. </li>

1276<li>

1277<strong>

1278Internal Validity</strong>

1279: Good.</li>

1280<li>

1281<strong>

1282Consistency</strong>

1283: Good.</li>

1284<li>

1285<strong>

1286External Validity</strong>

1287: Good.</li>

1288</ul>

1289</div>

1290</section>

1291<section id="_810">

1292<h4 id="_810_toc">

1293Estrogen therapy</h4>

1294<p id="_811" tabindex="-1">

1295Based on solid evidence, estrogen therapy that began close to the time of menopause is associated with an increased risk of breast cancer. Estrogen therapy that began at or after menopause is associated with an increased risk of endometrial cancer and total cardiovascular disease, especially stroke.</p>

1296<p id="_812" tabindex="-1">

1297<strong>

1298Magnitude of Effect</strong>

1299: The increased incidence of breast cancer associated with estrogen therapy that began at the time of menopause ranged from 17% to 33%, depending on duration of use. Breast cancer incidence in women who have undergone hysterectomy is 23% lower if estrogen use began many years after menopause. There is a 39% increase in stroke (RR, 1.12; 95% confidence interval [CI], 1.1&#8211;1.77) and a 12% increase in cardiovascular disease (RR, 1.12; 95% CI, 1.01&#8211;1.24).[<a href="#cit/section_2.3">

13003</a>

1301]</p>

1302<div class="pdq-content-list">

1303<ul id="_813" class="pdq-address-block">

1304<li>

1305<strong>

1306Study Design</strong>

1307: RCTs and ecological observations.</li>

1308<li>

1309<strong>

1310Internal Validity</strong>

1311: Good.</li>

1312<li>

1313<strong>

1314Consistency</strong>

1315: Good, although in women who have undergone hysterectomy, estrogen use that began many years after menopause was associated with a decrease in breast cancer incidence.</li>

1316<li>

1317<strong>

1318External Validity</strong>

1319: Good.</li>

1320</ul>

1321</div>

1322</section>

1323<section id="_579">

1324<h4 id="_579_toc">

1325Ionizing radiation</h4>

1326<p id="_580" tabindex="-1">

1327Based on solid evidence, exposure of the breast to ionizing radiation is associated with an increased risk of developing breast cancer, starting 10 years after exposure and persisting lifelong. Risk depends on radiation dose and age at exposure, and is especially high if exposure occurs during puberty, when the breast develops.</p>

1328<p id="_581" tabindex="-1">

1329<strong>

1330Magnitude of Effect</strong>

1331: Variable but approximately a sixfold increase overall. </p>

1332<div class="pdq-content-list">

1333<ul id="_582" class="pdq-address-block">

1334<li>

1335<strong>

1336Study Design</strong>

1337: Cohort or case-control studies.</li>

1338<li>

1339<strong>

1340Internal Validity</strong>

1341: Good.</li>

1342<li>

1343<strong>

1344Consistency</strong>

1345: Good.</li>

1346<li>

1347<strong>

1348External Validity</strong>

1349: Good.</li>

1350</ul>

1351</div>

1352</section>

1353<section id="_583">

1354<h4 id="_583_toc">

1355Obesity</h4>

1356<p id="_584" tabindex="-1">

1357Based on solid evidence, obesity is associated with an increased breast cancer risk in postmenopausal women who have not used HT. It is uncertain whether weight reduction decreases the risk of breast cancer in obese women.</p>

1358<p id="_585" tabindex="-1">

1359<strong>

1360Magnitude of Effect</strong>

1361: The Women's Health Initiative observational study of 85,917 postmenopausal women found body weight to be associated with breast cancer. Comparing women weighing more than 82.2 kg with those weighing less than 58.7 kg, the RR was 2.85 (95% CI, 1.81&#8211;4.49).</p>

1362<div class="pdq-content-list">

1363<ul id="_586" class="pdq-address-block">

1364<li>

1365<strong>

1366Study Design</strong>

1367: Case-control and cohort studies.</li>

1368<li>

1369<strong>

1370Internal Validity</strong>

1371: Good.</li>

1372<li>

1373<strong>

1374Consistency</strong>

1375: Good.</li>

1376<li>

1377<strong>

1378External Validity</strong>

1379: Good.</li>

1380</ul>

1381</div>

1382</section>

1383<section id="_587">

1384<h4 id="_587_toc">

1385Alcohol</h4>

1386<p id="_588" tabindex="-1">

1387Based on solid evidence, alcohol consumption is associated with increased breast cancer risk in a dose-dependent fashion. It is uncertain whether decreasing alcohol intake by heavy drinkers reduces the risk.</p>

1388<p id="_589" tabindex="-1">

1389<strong>

1390Magnitude of Effect</strong>

1391: The RR for women consuming approximately four alcoholic drinks per day compared with nondrinkers is 1.32 (95% CI, 1.19&#8211;1.45). The RR increases by 7% (95% CI, 5.5%&#8211;8.7%) for each drink per day.</p>

1392<div class="pdq-content-list">

1393<ul id="_590" class="pdq-address-block">

1394<li>

1395<strong>

1396Study Design</strong>

1397: Case-control and cohort studies.</li>

1398<li>

1399<strong>

1400Internal Validity</strong>

1401: Good.</li>

1402<li>

1403<strong>

1404Consistency</strong>

1405: Good.</li>

1406<li>

1407<strong>

1408External Validity</strong>

1409: Good.</li>

1410</ul>

1411</div>

1412</section>

1413</section>

1414<section id="_366">

1415<h3 id="_366_toc">

1416Factors With Adequate Evidence of Decreased Risk of Breast Cancer</h3>

1417<section id="_591">

1418<h4 id="_591_toc">

1419Early pregnancy</h4>

1420<p id="_592" tabindex="-1">

1421Based on solid evidence, women who have a full-term pregnancy before age 20 years have decreased breast cancer risk. </p>

1422<p id="_593" tabindex="-1">

1423<strong>

1424Magnitude of Effect</strong>

1425: 50% decrease in breast cancer, compared with nulliparous women or women who give birth after age 35 years.</p>

1426<div class="pdq-content-list">

1427<ul id="_594" class="pdq-address-block">

1428<li>

1429<strong>

1430Study Design</strong>

1431: Case-control and cohort studies.</li>

1432<li>

1433<strong>

1434Internal Validity</strong>

1435: Good.</li>

1436<li>

1437<strong>

1438Consistency</strong>

1439: Good.</li>

1440<li>

1441<strong>

1442External Validity</strong>

1443: Good.</li>

1444</ul>

1445</div>

1446</section>

1447<section id="_595">

1448<h4 id="_595_toc">

1449Breast-feeding</h4>

1450<p id="_596" tabindex="-1">

1451Based on solid evidence, women who breast-feed have a decreased risk of breast cancer.</p>

1452<p id="_597" tabindex="-1">

1453<strong>

1454Magnitude of Effect</strong>

1455: The RR of breast cancer is decreased 4.3% for every 12 months of breast-feeding, in addition to 7% for each birth.[<a href="#cit/section_2.4">

14564</a>

1457]</p>

1458<div class="pdq-content-list">

1459<ul id="_598" class="pdq-address-block">

1460<li>

1461<strong>

1462Study Design</strong>

1463: Case-control and cohort studies.</li>

1464<li>

1465<strong>

1466Internal Validity</strong>

1467: Good.</li>

1468<li>

1469<strong>

1470Consistency</strong>

1471: Good.</li>

1472<li>

1473<strong>

1474External Validity</strong>

1475: Good.</li>

1476</ul>

1477</div>

1478</section>

1479<section id="_599">

1480<h4 id="_599_toc">

1481Exercise</h4>

1482<p id="_600" tabindex="-1">

1483Based on solid evidence, physical exercise is associated with reduced breast cancer risk.</p>

1484<p id="_601" tabindex="-1">

1485<strong>

1486Magnitude of Effect</strong>

1487: Average RR reduction association is 20% for both postmenopausal and premenopausal women and affects the risk of both hormone-sensitive and hormone-resistant cancers.</p>

1488<div class="pdq-content-list">

1489<ul id="_602" class="pdq-address-block">

1490<li>

1491<strong>

1492Study Design</strong>

1493: Prospective observational and retrospective studies.</li>

1494<li>

1495<strong>

1496Internal Validity</strong>

1497: Good.</li>

1498<li>

1499<strong>

1500Consistency</strong>

1501: Good.</li>

1502<li>

1503<strong>

1504External Validity</strong>

1505: Good.</li>

1506</ul>

1507</div>

1508</section>

1509</section>

1510<section id="_115">

1511<h3 id="_115_toc">

1512Interventions With Adequate Evidence of Decreased Risk of Breast Cancer</h3>

1513<section id="_184">

1514<h4 id="_184_toc">

1515 Selective estrogen receptor modulators (SERMs): benefits</h4>

1516<p id="_250" tabindex="-1">

1517Based on solid evidence, tamoxifen and raloxifene reduce the incidence of breast cancer in postmenopausal women, and tamoxifen reduces the risk of breast cancer in high-risk premenopausal women. The effects observed for tamoxifen and raloxifene persist several years after active treatment is discontinued, with longer duration of effect noted for tamoxifen than for raloxifene.[<a href="#cit/section_2.5">

15185</a>

1519]</p>

1520<p id="_547" tabindex="-1">

1521All fractures were reduced by SERMs, primarily noted with raloxifene but not with tamoxifen. Reductions in vertebral fractures (34% reduction) and small reductions in nonvertebral fractures (7%) were noted.[<a href="#cit/section_2.5">

15225</a>

1523]</p>

1524<p id="_386" tabindex="-1">

1525<strong>

1526Magnitude of Effect</strong>

1527: Tamoxifen reduced the incidence of estrogen receptor&#8211;positive (ER-positive) breast cancer and ductal carcinoma <em>

1528in situ</em>

1529 (DCIS) in high-risk women by about 30% to 50% over 5 years of treatment. The reduction in ER-positive invasive breast cancer was maintained for at least 16 years after starting treatment (11 years after tamoxifen cessation). Breast cancer mortality was not affected.[<a href="#cit/section_2.6">

15306</a>

1531]</p>

1532<div class="pdq-content-list">

1533<ul id="_251" class="pdq-address-block">

1534<li>

1535<strong>

1536Study Design</strong>

1537: RCTs.</li>

1538<li>

1539<strong>

1540Internal Validity</strong>

1541: Good.</li>

1542<li>

1543<strong>

1544Consistency</strong>

1545: Good.</li>

1546<li>

1547<strong>

1548External Validity</strong>

1549: Good.</li>

1550</ul>

1551</div>

1552</section>

1553<section id="_766">

1554<h4 id="_766_toc">

1555Selective estrogen receptor modulators: harms</h4>

1556<p id="_609" tabindex="-1">

1557Based on solid evidence, tamoxifen increases the risk of endometrial cancer, thrombotic vascular events (i.e., pulmonary embolism, stroke, and deep venous thrombosis), and cataracts. The endometrial cancer risk persists for 5 years after tamoxifen cessation but not the risk of vascular events or cataracts. Based on solid evidence, raloxifene also increases venous pulmonary embolism and deep venous thrombosis but not endometrial cancer.</p>

1558<p id="_610" tabindex="-1">

1559<strong>

1560Magnitude of Effect</strong>

1561: Meta-analysis showed RR of 2.4 (95% CI, 1.5&#8211;4.0) for endometrial cancer and 1.9 (95% CI, 1.4&#8211;2.6) for venous thromboembolic events. Meta-analysis showed the hazard ratio (HR) for endometrial cancer was 2.18 (95% CI, 1.39&#8211;3.42) for tamoxifen and 1.09 (95% CI, 0.74&#8211;1.62) for raloxifene. Overall, HR for venous thromboembolic events was 1.73 (95% CI, 1.47&#8211;2.05). Harms were significantly higher in women over 50 years than in younger women.</p>

1562<div class="pdq-content-list">

1563<ul id="_611" class="pdq-address-block">

1564<li>

1565<strong>

1566Study Design</strong>

1567: RCTs.</li>

1568<li>

1569<strong>

1570Internal Validity</strong>

1571: Good.</li>

1572<li>

1573<strong>

1574Consistency</strong>

1575: Good.</li>

1576<li>

1577<strong>

1578External Validity</strong>

1579: Good.</li>

1580</ul>

1581</div>

1582</section>

1583<section id="_260">

1584<h4 id="_260_toc">

1585Aromatase inhibitors or inactivators: benefits</h4>

1586<p id="_261" tabindex="-1">

1587Based on solid evidence, aromatase inhibitors or inactivators (AIs) reduce breast cancer incidence in postmenopausal women who have an increased risk.</p>

1588<p id="_388" tabindex="-1">

1589<strong>

1590Magnitude of Effect</strong>

1591: In postmenopausal women treated with adjuvant tamoxifen for hormone-sensitive breast cancer, subsequent therapy with AIs reduced the incidence of new primary breast cancers by 50% to 67%, compared with controls. In postmenopausal women at high risk of developing breast cancer, 3 years of exemestane treatment reduced breast cancer incidence by 65%, compared with controls. A similar trial of 5 years of anastrozole treatment reduced breast cancer incidence by 53%, an effect persisting at 11 years.[<a href="#cit/section_2.7">

15927</a>

1593] After a median follow-up of 35 months, women aged 35 years and older who had at least one risk factor (age &gt;60 years, a Gail 5-year risk &gt;1.66%, or DCIS with mastectomy) and who took 25 mg of exemestane daily had a decreased risk of invasive breast cancer (HR, 0.35; 95% CI, 0.18&#8211;0.70) compared with controls. The absolute risk reduction was 21 cancers avoided out of 2,280 participants over 35 months. The number needed to treat was about 100.[<a href="#cit/section_2.8">

15948</a>

1595]</p>

1596<div class="pdq-content-list">

1597<ul id="_291" class="pdq-address-block">

1598<li>

1599<strong>

1600Study Design</strong>

1601: Multiple RCTs.</li>

1602<li>

1603<strong>

1604 Internal Validity</strong>

1605: Good.</li>

1606<li>

1607<strong>

1608Consistency</strong>

1609: Good.</li>

1610<li>

1611<strong>

1612External Validity</strong>

1613: Good.</li>

1614</ul>

1615</div>

1616</section>

1617<section id="_767">

1618<h4 id="_767_toc">

1619Aromatase inhibitors or inactivators: harms</h4>

1620<p id="_263" tabindex="-1">

1621Based on fair evidence from a single RCT of 4,560 women over 35 months, exemestane is associated with hot flashes and fatigue compared with placebo.[<a href="#cit/section_2.8">

16228</a>

1623,<a href="#cit/section_2.9">

16249</a>

1625]</p>

1626<p id="_389" tabindex="-1">

1627<strong>

1628Magnitude of Effect</strong>

1629: The absolute increase in hot flashes was 8% and the absolute increase in fatigue was 2%.</p>

1630<div class="pdq-content-list">

1631<ul id="_292" class="pdq-address-block">

1632<li>

1633<strong>

1634Study Design</strong>

1635: One RCT.</li>

1636<li>

1637<strong>

1638 Internal Validity</strong>

1639: Good.</li>

1640<li>

1641<strong>

1642Consistency</strong>

1643: Good.</li>

1644<li>

1645<strong>

1646External Validity</strong>

1647: Good for women who meet inclusion criteria.</li>

1648</ul>

1649</div>

1650</section>

1651<section id="_186">

1652<h4 id="_186_toc">

1653Prophylactic mastectomy: benefits</h4>

1654<p id="_187" tabindex="-1">

1655Based on solid evidence, bilateral prophylactic mastectomy reduces the risk of breast cancer in women with a strong family history, and most women experience relief from anxiety about breast cancer risk. Based on strong evidence, bilateral prophylactic mastectomy reduces the risk of breast cancer in women with a strong family history of breast cancer or other factors putting them at high risk (e.g., certain previous chest-wall radiation or previous personal history of breast cancer). Most women experience relief from anxiety about breast cancer risk after undergoing prophylactic mastectomy. Although some studies have suggested a survival benefit associated with contralateral prophylactic mastectomy, these results are generally attributed to selection bias, and there are no high-quality studies demonstrating a clear survival advantage.</p>

1656<p id="_390" tabindex="-1">

1657<strong>

1658Magnitude of Effect</strong>

1659: Breast cancer risk after bilateral prophylactic mastectomy in women at high risk may be reduced as much as 90%.</p>

1660<div class="pdq-content-list">

1661<ul id="_293" class="pdq-address-block">

1662<li>

1663<strong>

1664Study Design</strong>

1665: Evidence obtained from case-control and cohort studies.</li>

1666<li>

1667<strong>

1668 Internal Validity</strong>

1669: Good.</li>

1670<li>

1671<strong>

1672Consistency</strong>

1673: Good.</li>

1674<li>

1675<strong>

1676External Validity</strong>

1677: Good.</li>

1678</ul>

1679</div>

1680</section>

1681<section id="_266">

1682<h4 id="_266_toc">

1683Prophylactic oophorectomy or ovarian ablation: benefits</h4>

1684<p id="_267" tabindex="-1">

1685Based on solid evidence, prophylactic oophorectomy in premenopausal women with a <em class="gene-name">

1686BRCA</em>

1687 gene mutation is associated with decreased breast cancer incidence. Similar results are seen for oophorectomy or ovarian ablation in normal premenopausal women and in women with increased breast cancer risk resulting from thoracic irradiation. </p>

1688<p id="_392" tabindex="-1">

1689<strong>

1690Magnitude of Effect</strong>

1691: Breast cancer incidence may be decreased by up to 50%.</p>

1692<div class="pdq-content-list">

1693<ul id="_295" class="pdq-address-block">

1694<li>

1695<strong>

1696Study Design</strong>

1697: Observational, case-control, and cohort studies.</li>

1698<li>

1699 <strong>

1700Internal Validity</strong>

1701: Good.</li>

1702<li>

1703<strong>

1704Consistency</strong>

1705: Good.</li>

1706<li>

1707<strong>

1708External Validity</strong>

1709: Good.</li>

1710</ul>

1711</div>

1712</section>

1713<section id="_768">

1714<h4 id="_768_toc">

1715Prophylactic oophorectomy or ovarian ablation: harms</h4>

1716<p id="_617" tabindex="-1">

1717Based on solid evidence, castration may cause the abrupt onset of menopausal symptoms such as hot flashes, insomnia, anxiety, and depression. Long-term effects include decreased libido, vaginal dryness, and decreased bone mineral density.</p>

1718<p id="_618" tabindex="-1">

1719<strong>

1720Magnitude of Effect</strong>

1721: Nearly all women experience some sleep disturbances, mood changes, hot flashes, and bone demineralization, but the severity of these symptoms varies greatly. </p>

1722<div class="pdq-content-list">

1723<ul id="_619" class="pdq-address-block">

1724<li>

1725<strong>

1726Study Design</strong>

1727: Observational, case-control, and cohort studies.</li>

1728<li>

1729<strong>

1730 Internal Validity</strong>

1731: Good.</li>

1732<li>

1733<strong>

1734Consistency</strong>

1735: Good.</li>

1736<li>

1737<strong>

1738External Validity</strong>

1739: Good.</li>

1740</ul>

1741</div>

1790 <section id="_30">

1791

1792 <h2>

1793Incidence and Mortality</h2>

1794

1795 <div id="_section_30" class="pdq-sections">

1796<p id="_31" tabindex="-1">

1797Breast

1798cancer is the most frequently diagnosed nonskin malignancy in U.S. women and is second only to lung cancer in cancer deaths in women.[<a href="#cit/section_3.1">

17991</a>

1800] Estimates for the U.S. population in 2023 are that 297,790 women will be diagnosed with breast cancer, with 43,170 deaths from this disease, and 2,800 men will be diagnosed with breast cancer, with 530 deaths from this disease.[<a href="#cit/section_3.1">

18011</a>

1802] Breast cancer

1803incidence in women had been gradually increasing for many years until the early 2000s, when it decreased rapidly, coincident with a drop in postmenopausal hormone therapy use. However, since the initial decrease between 2000 and 2005, there has been a small but steady increase in incidence, approaching incidence rates seen before that decrease.[<a href="#cit/section_3.2">

18042</a>

1805]</p>

1806<p id="_827" tabindex="-1">

1807According to data from the Surveillance, Epidemiology, and End Results (SEER) Program, breast cancer mortality declined by 43% from 1989 to 2020; however, mortality rates in Black women remain about 40% higher than in White women.[<a href="#cit/section_3.1">

18081</a>

1809] Incidence rates are now similar between Black and White women. </p>

1810<p id="_624" tabindex="-1">

1811The major risk factor for breast cancer is advancing age. A 30-year-old woman has about a 1 in 200 chance of being diagnosed with breast cancer in the next 10 years, whereas a 70-year-old woman has a 1 in 25 chance.[<a href="#cit/section_3.2">

18122</a>

1813]</p>

1814<p id="_625" tabindex="-1">

1815Screening by mammography decreases breast cancer mortality by identifying cases for treatment at an earlier stage. However, screening also identifies more cases than would become symptomatic in a woman&#8217;s lifetime, so screening increases breast cancer incidence. For more information, see the <a href="/types/breast/hp/breast-screening-pdq#_666">

1816Overdiagnosis</a>

1817 section in Breast Cancer Screening.</p>

1818<h6 do-not-show="toc">

1819References</h6>

1820<ol>

1821<li id="section_3.1">

1823Available online</a>

1824. Last accessed June 8, 2023.</li>

1827Available online</a>

1828. Last accessed August 18, 2023.</li>

1836 <section id="_34">

1837

1838 <h2>

1839Etiology and Pathogenesis of Breast Cancer</h2>

1840

1841 <div id="_section_34" class="pdq-sections">

1842<p id="_35" tabindex="-1">

1843Breast cancer develops when a series of genetic mutations occurs.[<a href="#cit/section_4.1">

18441</a>

1845] Some cancer-associated mutations are inherited, but most are somatic mutations that occur as random events during a woman&#8217;s lifetime. Initially, mutations do not change the histological appearance of the tissue, but accumulated mutations will result in hyperplasia, dysplasia, carcinoma <em>

1846in situ</em>

1847, and eventually, invasive cancer.[<a href="#cit/section_4.2">

18482</a>

1849] The longer a woman lives, the more somatic mutations occur, and the more likely it is that these mutations will produce populations of cells that may eventually become malignancies. Estrogen and progestin hormones, whether endogenous or exogenous, stimulate growth and proliferation of breast cells, perhaps via growth factors such as transforming growth factor-alpha.[<a href="#cit/section_4.3">

18503</a>

1851] The stimulation by these hormones can promote the development and proliferation of breast cancer cells.

1852</p>

1853<p id="_36" tabindex="-1">

1854International variation in breast cancer rates may be explained by differences in genetics, reproductive factors, diet, exercise, and screening behavior. The relative importance of these factors was demonstrated in a study of breast cancer incidence of Japanese immigrants to the United States. Whereas Japanese women in Japan had a low breast cancer incidence, Japanese women in the United States had a much higher breast cancer incidence, similar to that of American women, within two generations of migration.[<a href="#cit/section_4.4">

18554</a>

1856-<a href="#cit/section_4.6">

18576</a>

1858]

1859</p>

1860<h6 do-not-show="toc">

1894 <section id="_37">

1895

1896 <h2>

1897Endogenous Estrogen</h2>

1898

1899 <div id="_section_37" class="pdq-sections">

1900<p id="_276" tabindex="-1">

1901Endogenous estrogen plays a role in the development of breast cancer. Women whose menarche occurred at or before age 11 years have about a 20% greater chance of developing breast cancer than do women whose menarche occurred at or after age 14 years.[<a href="#cit/section_5.1">

19021</a>

1903-<a href="#cit/section_5.3">

19043</a>

1905] Women who experience late menopause also have an increased risk. Women who develop breast cancer tend to have higher endogenous estrogen and androgen levels.[<a href="#cit/section_5.3">

19063</a>

1907-<a href="#cit/section_5.7">

19087</a>

1909]</p>

1910<p id="_38" tabindex="-1">

1911Conversely, women who experience premature menopause have a lower risk of breast cancer. Following ovarian ablation,

1912breast cancer risk may be reduced as much as 75% depending on age, weight, and parity, with the greatest reduction for young,

1913thin, nulliparous women.[<a href="#cit/section_5.8">

19148</a>

1915-<a href="#cit/section_5.11">

191611</a>

1917] The removal of one ovary

1918also reduces the risk of breast cancer but to a lesser degree.[<a href="#cit/section_5.12">

191912</a>

1920]

1921</p>

1922<p id="_39" tabindex="-1">

1923Other hormonal changes also

1924influence breast cancer risk. For more information, see the sections on <a href="/types/breast/hp/breast-prevention-pdq#_428">

1925Early Pregnancy</a>

1926 and <a href="/types/breast/hp/breast-prevention-pdq#_430">

1927Breast-feeding</a>

1928 in the Factors With Adequate Evidence of Decreased Risk of Breast Cancer section.</p>

1929<p id="_626" tabindex="-1">

1930The interaction of endogenous estrogen levels, insulin levels, and obesity&#8212;all of which affect breast cancer risk&#8212;are poorly understood but suggest strategies for interventions to decrease that risk. It is likely that reproductive risk factors interact with predisposing genotypes. For example, in the Nurses&#8217; Health Study,[<a href="#cit/section_5.13">

193113</a>

1932] the associations between age at first birth, menarche, and menopause and the development of breast cancer were observed only among women without a family history of breast cancer in a mother or sister.</p>

1995 <section id="_701">

1996

1997 <h2>

1998Inherited Risk</h2>

1999

2000 <div id="_section_701" class="pdq-sections">

2001<p id="_702" tabindex="-1">

2002Breast cancer risk increases in women with a positive family history, particularly if first-degree relatives are affected.[<a href="#cit/section_6.1">

20031</a>

2004] The following risk assessment models, derived from databases, cohort, and case-control studies, quantitate this risk:</p>

2005<div class="pdq-content-list">

2006<ul id="_741">

2007<li>

2008<a href="https://bcrisktool.cancer.gov" title="https://bcrisktool.cancer.gov">

2009Breast Cancer Risk Assessment Tool [Gail Model]</a>

2010.</li>

2011<li>

2012<a href="https://tools.bcsc-scc.org/BC5yearRisk/intro.htm" title="https://tools.bcsc-scc.org/BC5yearRisk/intro.htm">

2013Breast Cancer Surveillance Consortium [BCSC] Risk Calculator</a>

2014.</li>

2015<li>

2016<a href="https://www.breastcancergenescreen.org/" title="https://www.breastcancergenescreen.org/">

2017Breast Cancer Referral Screening Tool [B-RST]</a>

2018.</li>

2019<li>

2020<a href="http://halls.md/breast/risk.htm" title="http://halls.md/breast/risk.htm">

2021Hall Detailed Breast Risk Calculator</a>

2022.</li>

2023<li>

2024<a href="http://www.ems-trials.org/riskevaluator/" title="http://www.ems-trials.org/riskevaluator/">

2025IBIS Breast Cancer Risk Calculator Tool</a>

2026.</li>

2027</ul>

2028</div>

2029<p id="_742" tabindex="-1">

2030Specific abnormal alleles are associated with approximately 5% of breast cancers. For more information, see <a href="/types/breast/hp/breast-ovarian-genetics-pdq">

2031Genetics of Breast and Gynecologic Cancers</a>

2032. Mutations in <em class="gene-name">

2033BRCA</em>

2034 genes are inherited in an autosomal dominant fashion and are highly penetrant in causing cancer, often at a younger age.[<a href="#cit/section_6.2">

20352</a>

2036-<a href="#cit/section_6.4">

20374</a>

2038] Family history and mutation location within the <em class="gene-name">

2039BRCA1</em>

2040 or <em class="gene-name">

2041BRCA2</em>

2042 gene may contribute to the risk of cancer development among those with an inherited predisposition to breast cancer.[<a href="#cit/section_6.5">

20435</a>

2044] The lifetime risk of breast cancer is 55% to 65% for <em class="gene-name">

2045BRCA1</em>

2046 mutation carriers and 45% to 47% for <em class="gene-name">

2047BRCA2</em>

2048 mutation carriers.[<a href="#cit/section_6.6">

20496</a>

2050,<a href="#cit/section_6.7">

20517</a>

2052] In comparison, the lifetime risk of breast cancer is 13% in the general population.[<a href="#cit/section_6.8">

20538</a>

2054]</p>

2055<p id="_743" tabindex="-1">

2056Some women inherit a susceptibility to mutagens or growth factors, which increase breast cancer risk.[<a href="#cit/section_6.9">

20579</a>

2058,<a href="#cit/section_6.10">

205910</a>

2060] For more information, see the <a href="/types/breast/hp/breast-prevention-pdq#_149">

2061Ionizing Radiation Exposure</a>

2062 section.</p>

2112

2113 <section id="_703">

2114

2115 <h2>

2116Increased Breast Density</h2>

2117

2118 <div id="_section_703" class="pdq-sections">

2119<p id="_704" tabindex="-1">

2120Widespread use of screening mammograms has demonstrated great variability in breast tissue density. Women with a greater proportion of dense tissue have a higher incidence of breast cancer. Mammographic density also confounds the identification of cancers by mammograms. The extent of increased risk was described in a report of three nested case-control studies in screened populations with 1,112 matched case-control pairs. Compared with women with density comprising less than 10% of breast tissue, women with density in 75% or more of their breast had an increased risk of breast cancer (odds ratio [OR], 4.7; 95% confidence interval [CI], 3.0&#8211;7.4), whether the cancer was detected by screening (OR, 3.5; 95% CI, 2.0&#8211;6.2) or detected less than 12 months after a negative screening examination (OR, 17.8; 95% CI, 4.8&#8211;65.9). Increased risk of breast cancer, whether detected by screening or other means, persisted for at least 8 years after study entry and was greater in younger women than in older women. For women younger than the median age of 56 years, 26% of all breast cancers and 50% of cancers detected less than 12 months after a negative screening test were identified in women with mammographic breast density of 50% or more.[<a href="#cit/section_7.1">

21211</a>

2122,<a href="#cit/section_7.2">

21232</a>

2124]</p>

2125<p id="_705" tabindex="-1">

2126Compared with women who have the lowest breast density, women with dense breasts have increased risk, proportionate to the degree of density. This increased relative risk ranges from 1.79 for women with slightly increased breast density to 4.64 for women with very dense breasts.[<a href="#cit/section_7.3">

21273</a>

2128] There is no increased risk of breast cancer mortality among women with dense breast tissue.[<a href="#cit/section_7.4">

21294</a>

2130]</p>

2131<h6 do-not-show="toc">

2132References</h6>

N Engl J Med 356 (3): 227-36, 2007.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=17229950&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=17229950&amp;dopt=Abstract">

2136[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

2159 <h2>

2160Factors With Adequate Evidence of Increased Risk of Breast Cancer</h2>

2161

2162 <div id="_section_410" class="pdq-sections">

2163<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

2164<h6>

2165In This Section</h6>

2169Menopausal Hormone Therapy (MHT)</a>

2170</li>

2171<li>

2172<a href="#_149_toc">

2173Ionizing Radiation Exposure</a>

2185<section id="_796">

2186<h3 id="_796_toc">

2187Menopausal Hormone Therapy (MHT)</h3>

2188<p id="_797" tabindex="-1">

2189MHT has been used to alleviate hot flashes and other symptoms associated with menopause. Single-agent estrogen is associated with an increased incidence of uterine cancer, but estrogen-progesterone use is not. Women with intact uteri are prescribed the combination, with oral progesterone given continuously or intermittently, or intrauterine progesterone delivered locally by intrauterine device (IUD). Estrogen therapy that began close to the time of menopause is associated with an increased risk of developing breast cancer. Estrogen therapy that began at or after menopause is associated with an increased risk of developing endometrial cancer and total cardiovascular disease, especially stroke.[<a href="#cit/section_8.1">

21901</a>

2191] Women who have undergone hysterectomy often take unopposed estrogen.</p>

2192<p id="_798" tabindex="-1">

2193In 1997, 51 epidemiological studies were reanalyzed, encompassing more than 150,000 women, and it was found that MHT was associated with increased breast cancer risk.[<a href="#cit/section_8.2">

21942</a>

2195]</p>

2196<p id="_799" tabindex="-1">

2197The Heart and Estrogen/Progestin Replacement Study, published in 2002, extended these findings by randomly assigning 2,763 women, with coronary heart disease (median age, 67), to receive either estrogen-progestin or placebo.[<a href="#cit/section_8.3">

21983</a>

2199] At 6.8 years of follow-up, breast cancer incidence was higher for hormone users (relative risk [RR], 1.27; 95% confidence interval [CI], 0.84&#8211;1.94).</p>

2200<p id="_800" tabindex="-1">

2201The Women&#8217;s Health Initiative (WHI), also published in 2002, randomly assigned over 16,000 women aged 50 to 79 years with intact uteri to receive either estrogen-progesterone or placebo.[<a href="#cit/section_8.4">

22024</a>

2203] The WHI was terminated early because the risk for coronary heart disease was unchanged, but the risk for stroke was increased with MHT. The incidence of invasive breast cancer was also increased in women who had taken MHT (hazard ratio, 1.24; 95% CI, 10.2&#8211;1.50). In addition to the randomized trial, the WHI conducted an observational study that examined women aged 50 to 79 years and found an increased risk of breast cancer, especially for those starting MHT at menopause. The WHI also randomly assigned 10,739 women who had undergone hysterectomy and were aged 50 to 79 years to receive either estrogen or placebo. Estrogen use was associated with an increased risk of stroke, so the trial was stopped early. After the publication of the WHI results, MHT use dropped worldwide, and breast cancer risk declined in countries where MHT usage had been high.</p>

2204<p id="_801" tabindex="-1">

2205In 2003, the Cancer Surveillance System of Puget Sound reported results of a population-based survey of 965 women with breast cancer and 1,007 controls.[<a href="#cit/section_8.5">

22065</a>

2207] This study showed a 1.7-fold increased risk of invasive breast cancer with estrogen-progesterone use, but not with estrogen use alone.</p>

2208<p id="_802" tabindex="-1">

2209While the association between estrogen-progesterone MHT and breast cancer risk was consistently observed, questions arose about the use of estrogen-only in women who had undergone hysterectomy, especially about the timing of therapy in relation to menopause and the participation in screening activities by MHT users.</p>

2210<p id="_808" tabindex="-1">

2211The United Kingdom Million Women Study [<a href="#cit/section_8.6">

22126</a>

2213] recruited 1,084,110 women aged 50 to 64 years between 1996 and 2001. This study obtained information about MHT use, along with other health information, and followed them for breast cancer incidence and death. One-half of the women had used MHT. At 2.6 years of follow up, there were 9,364 invasive breast cancers, and at 4.1 years, there were 637 breast cancer deaths. At recruitment, current MHT users were more likely than were never-users to develop breast cancer (adjusted RR, 1.66; 95% CI, 1.58&#8211;1.75; <em>

2214P</em>

2215 &lt; .0001) and to die from the disease (adjusted RR, 1.22; 95% CI, 1.00&#8211;1.48; <em>

2216P</em>

2217 = .05). Past MHT users had no increased risk of incident breast cancer (odds ratio [OR], 1.01; 95% CI, 0.94&#8211;1.09) or fatal breast cancer (OR, 1.05; 95% CI, 0.82&#8211;1.34). Incidence was increased for current users of estrogen (RR, 1.30; 95% CI, 1.21&#8211;1.40; <em>

2218P</em>

2219 &lt; .0001), combined MHT (RR, 2.00; 95% CI, 1.88&#8211;2.12; <em>

2220P</em>

2221 &lt; .0001), and tibolone (RR, 1.45; 95% CI, 1.25&#8211;1.68; <em>

2222P</em>

2223 &lt; .0001). The magnitude of the associated risk was greater for combined MHT than for other types of MHT (<em>

2224P</em>

2225 &lt; .0001). Tibolone is approved for use to manage menopausal symptoms or to prevent osteoporosis in many countries. However, it is not approved for use in Canada or the United States.</p>

2226<p id="_809" tabindex="-1">

2227A population-based survey of 965 women with breast cancer and 1,007 controls was conducted by the Cancer Surveillance System of Puget Sound. It showed that combined MHT users had a 1.7-fold increased risk of invasive breast cancer, whereas estrogen-only users did not.[<a href="#cit/section_8.5">

22285</a>

2229]</p>

2230<p id="_804" tabindex="-1">

2231In 2019, the Collaborative Group on Hormonal Factors in Breast Cancer reported results of a meta-analysis of 24 prospective and 34 retrospective studies of MHT and breast cancer risk, encompassing 143,887 women who developed breast cancer and 424,972 controls. Overall, MHT users had higher breast cancer risk, especially for hormone-sensitive tumors.[<a href="#cit/section_8.7">

22327</a>

2233]</p>

2234<table id="_805" class="table-default expandable-container">

2235<caption>

2236Table 1. Breast Cancer Risk Among Menopausal Hormone Therapy Users<span class="sup">

2237a</span>

2238</caption>

2291<p id="_806" tabindex="-1">

2292 The associations between MHT and breast cancer were less for women starting MHT after age 60 years and for obese women (obese women had minimal risk from estrogen MHT that began after age 60 years). In summary, women of average weight who used 5 years of MHT starting at 50 years have an increased risk of breast cancer incidence of 1 in 50 users for estrogen-progesterone, 1 in 70 users for estrogen plus intermittent progesterone, and 1 in 200 users for estrogen-only products.</p>

2293<p id="_807" tabindex="-1">

2294This meta-analysis, confirming and expanding the understanding of estrogen-progesterone risk of breast cancer, also resolves the question of estrogen use in women who have undergone hysterectomy. If the estrogen use began at menopause, it is associated with an increase in breast cancer risk, but not if it began many years later. These findings, as well as the documented increase in stroke risk with estrogen, should be considered when reviewing options to treat.</p>

2295</section>

2296<section id="_149">

2297<h3 id="_149_toc">

2298Ionizing Radiation Exposure</h3>

2299<p id="_150" tabindex="-1">

2300A well-established relationship exists between exposure to ionizing radiation

2301and subsequent breast cancer.[<a href="#cit/section_8.8">

23028</a>

2303] Excess breast cancer risk has been observed in association with atomic bomb exposure, frequent

2304fluoroscopy for tuberculosis, and radiation therapy for acne, tinea, thymic

2305enlargement, postpartum mastitis, and lymphoma. Risk is higher for the young, especially around puberty. An

2306estimate of the risk of breast cancer associated with medical radiology puts

2307the figure at less than 1% of all breast cancer cases.[<a href="#cit/section_8.9">

23089</a>

2309] However, it has been theorized that

2310certain populations, such as <em class="gene-name">

2311AT</em>

2312 heterozygotes, are at

2313an increased risk of breast cancer from radiation exposure.[<a href="#cit/section_8.10">

231410</a>

2315] A large cohort study of women who carry mutations of <em class="gene-name">

2316BRCA1</em>

2317 or <em class="gene-name">

2318BRCA2</em>

2319 concluded that chest x-rays, especially before age 20 years increased their risk of breast cancer beyond already increased levels (RR, 1.54; 95% CI, 1.1&#8211;2.1).[<a href="#cit/section_8.11">

232011</a>

2321]</p>

2322<p id="_151" tabindex="-1">

2323Women treated for Hodgkin lymphoma with mantle radiation by age 16 years have a subsequent risk up to 35% of

2324developing breast cancer by age 40 years.[<a href="#cit/section_8.12">

232512</a>

2326-<a href="#cit/section_8.14">

232714</a>

2328] Higher radiation doses (median dose, 40 Gy in breast cancer cases) and

2329treatment between the ages of 10 and 16 years are associated with higher risk.[<a href="#cit/section_8.12">

233012</a>

2331] Unlike the risk for secondary leukemia, the risk of

2332treatment-related breast cancer does not abate with duration of follow-up, persisting more than 25 years after treatment.[<a href="#cit/section_8.12">

233312</a>

2334,<a href="#cit/section_8.14">

233514</a>

2336,<a href="#cit/section_8.15">

233715</a>

2338]

2339In these studies, most patients (85%&#8211;100%) who developed

2340breast cancer did so either within the field of radiation or at the

2341margin.[<a href="#cit/section_8.12">

234212</a>

2343,<a href="#cit/section_8.13">

234413</a>

2345,<a href="#cit/section_8.15">

234615</a>

2347] A Dutch study examined 48 women who developed breast cancer at least 5 years after treatment for Hodgkin disease and compared them with 175 matched female Hodgkin disease patients who did not develop breast cancer. Patients treated with chemotherapy and mantle radiation were less likely to develop breast cancer than were those treated with mantle radiation alone, possibly because of chemotherapy-induced ovarian suppression (RR, 0.06; 95% CI, 0.01&#8211;0.45).[<a href="#cit/section_8.16">

234816</a>

2349] Another study of 105 radiation-associated breast cancer patients and 266 age-matched and radiation-matched controls showed a similar protective effect for ovarian radiation.[<a href="#cit/section_8.14">

235014</a>

2351] These studies suggest that reduction of ovarian hormones limits the proliferation of breast tissue with radiation-induced mutations.[<a href="#cit/section_8.14">

235214</a>

2353] </p>

2354<p id="_152" tabindex="-1">

2355The question arises whether breast cancer patients treated with lumpectomy and radiation therapy

2356(L-RT) are at higher risk for second breast malignancies or other malignancies

2357than are those treated by mastectomy. Outcomes of 1,029 L-RT patients

2358were compared with outcomes of 1,387 patients who underwent mastectomies.

2359After a median follow-up of 15 years, there was no difference in the risk of

2360second malignancies.[<a href="#cit/section_8.17">

236117</a>

2362] Further evidence from three RCTs is also reassuring. One report of 1,851 women randomly assigned to undergo total mastectomy, lumpectomy alone, or L-RT showed rates of contralateral breast cancer to be 8.5%, 8.8%, and 9.4%, respectively.[<a href="#cit/section_8.18">

236318</a>

2364] Another study of 701 women randomly assigned to undergo radical mastectomy or breast-conserving surgery followed by radiation therapy demonstrated the rate of contralateral breast carcinomas per 100 woman-years to be 10.2 versus 8.7, respectively.[<a href="#cit/section_8.19">

236519</a>

2366] The third study compared 25-year outcomes of 1,665 women randomly assigned to undergo radical mastectomy, total mastectomy, or total mastectomy with radiation. There was no significant difference in the rate of contralateral breast cancer according to treatment group, and the overall rate was 6%.[<a href="#cit/section_8.20">

236720</a>

2368]</p>

2369</section>

2370<section id="_153">

2371<h3 id="_153_toc">

2372Obesity</h3>

2373<p id="_154" tabindex="-1">

2374Obesity is associated with increased breast cancer risk, especially among postmenopausal women who do not use hormone therapy (HT). The WHI observed 85,917 women aged 50 to 79 years and collected information on weight history and known risk factors for breast cancer.[<a href="#cit/section_8.21">

237521</a>

2376,<a href="#cit/section_8.22">

237722</a>

2378] Height, weight, and waist and hip circumferences were measured. With a median follow-up of 34.8 months, 1,030 of the women developed invasive breast cancer. Among the women who never used HT, increased breast cancer risk was associated with weight at entry, body mass index (BMI) at entry, BMI at age 50 years, maximum BMI, adult and postmenopausal weight change, and waist and hip circumferences. Weight was the strongest predictor, with a RR of 2.85 (95% CI, 1.81&#8211;4.49) for women weighing more than 82.2 kg, compared with those weighing less than 58.7 kg.</p>

2379<p id="_630" tabindex="-1">

2380The association between obesity, diabetes, and insulin levels with breast cancer risk have been studied but not clearly defined. The British Women&#8217;s Heart and Health Study of women aged 60 to 79 years compared 151 women who had a diagnosis of breast cancer with 3,690 women who did not. The age-adjusted OR was 1.34 (95% CI, 1.02&#8211;1.77) for each unit increase in log(e) insulin level among nondiabetic women. The association was observed, after adjustment for confounders and for potential mediating factors, for both pre- and postmenopausal breast cancers. In addition, fasting glucose level, homeostatic model assessment score (the product of fasting glucose and insulin levels divided by 22.5), diabetes, and a history of gestational glycosuria or diabetes were also associated with breast cancer.[<a href="#cit/section_8.23">

238123</a>

2382]</p>

2383</section>

2384<section id="_155">

2385<h3 id="_155_toc">

2386Alcohol</h3>

2387<p id="_156" tabindex="-1">

2388Alcohol consumption increases the risk of breast cancer. A British meta-analysis included individual data from 53 case-control and cohort studies.[<a href="#cit/section_8.24">

238924</a>

2390] Compared with the RR of breast cancer for women who reported no alcohol consumption, the RR of breast cancer was 1.32 (95% CI, 1.19&#8211;1.45; <em>

2391P</em>

2392 &lt; .001) for women consuming 35 g to 44 g of alcohol per day and 1.46 (95% CI, 1.33&#8211;1.61; <em>

2393P</em>

2394 &lt; .001) for those consuming at least 45 g of alcohol per day. The RR of breast cancer increases by about 7% (95% CI, 5.5%&#8211;8.7%; <em>

2395P</em>

2396 &lt; .001) for each 10 g of alcohol (i.e., one drink) consumed per day. These findings persist after stratification for race, education, family history, age at menarche, height, weight, BMI, breast-feeding, oral contraceptive use, menopausal hormone use and type, and age at menopause.</p>

2397</section>

2504 <section id="_423">

2505

2506 <h2>

2507Factors With Adequate Evidence of Decreased Risk of Breast Cancer</h2>

2508

2509 <div id="_section_423" class="pdq-sections">

2510<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

2511<h6>

2512In This Section</h6>

2513<ul>

2514<li>

2515<a href="#_428_toc">

2516Early Pregnancy</a>

2517</li>

2518<li>

2519<a href="#_430_toc">

2520Breast-feeding</a>

2521</li>

2522<li>

2523<a href="#_426_toc">

2524Exercise</a>

2525</li>

2526</ul>

2527</nav>

2528<section id="_428">

2529<h3 id="_428_toc">

2530Early Pregnancy</h3>

2531<p id="_429" tabindex="-1">

2532Childbirth is followed by an increase in risk of breast cancer for several years, and then a long-term reduction in risk, which is greater for younger women.[<a href="#cit/section_9.1">

25331</a>

2534-<a href="#cit/section_9.3">

25353</a>

2536] In one study, women who experienced a first full-term pregnancy before age 20 years were half as likely to develop breast cancer as nulliparous women or women whose first full-term pregnancy occurred at age 35 years or older.[<a href="#cit/section_9.4">

25374</a>

2538,<a href="#cit/section_9.5">

25395</a>

2540] </p>

2541<p id="_770" tabindex="-1">

2542The effect of childbirth on breast cancer risk was demonstrated by the International Premenopausal Breast Cancer Collaborative Group, which undertook a pooled analysis of individual-level data from about 890,000 women from 15 prospective cohort studies. When compared with nulliparous women, parous women had an increased risk of developing both estrogen receptor&#8211;positive (ER&#8211;positive) and estrogen receptor&#8211;negative (ER&#8211;negative) breast cancer for up to 20 years after childbirth. However, after about 24 years, the risk of developing ER&#8211;positive breast cancer decreased, but the risk of developing ER&#8211;negative breast cancer remained elevated. Thus, the association between parity and breast cancer risk is complex and appears to be influenced by the time period after childbirth, as well as tumor phenotype.[<a href="#cit/section_9.6">

25436</a>

2544]</p>

2545</section>

2546<section id="_430">

2547<h3 id="_430_toc">

2548Breast-feeding</h3>

2549<p id="_431" tabindex="-1">

2550Breast-feeding is associated with a decreased risk of breast cancer.[<a href="#cit/section_9.7">

25517</a>

2552] A reanalysis of individual data from 47 epidemiological studies in 30 countries of 50,302 women with breast cancer and 96,973 controls revealed that breast cancer incidence was lower in parous women who had ever breast-fed than in parous women who had not. It was also proportionate to duration of breast-feeding.[<a href="#cit/section_9.8">

25538</a>

2554] The relative risk (RR) of breast cancer decreased by 4.3% (95%, confidence interval [CI], 2.9%&#8211;5.8%; <em>

2555P</em>

2556 &lt; .0001) for every 12 months of breast-feeding in addition to a decrease of 7.0% (95% CI, 5.0%&#8211;9.0%; <em>

2557P</em>

2558 &lt; .0001) for each birth.</p>

2559</section>

2560<section id="_426">

2561<h3 id="_426_toc">

2562Exercise</h3>

2563<p id="_790" tabindex="-1">

2564Many studies have shown an associated benefit between physical exercise and breast cancer risk. A French study of 59,308 women, averaging 8.5 years postmenopause, found that recreational activity greater than 12 metabolic equivalent task (MET) h/wk was associated with decreased risk of invasive breast cancer (hazard ratio [HR], 0.9; 95% CI, 0.83&#8211;0.99).[<a href="#cit/section_9.9">

25659</a>

2566] The Nurses' Health Study included 95,396 postmenopausal women and found that women who exercised more than 27 MET h/wk (equivalent to 1 h/d of brisk walking) had decreased breast cancer incidence compared with those who had fewer than 3 MET h/wk (HR, 0.85; 95% CI, 0.78&#8211;0.93; <em>

2567P</em>

2568 &lt; .001 for trend). There was no difference in the association between exercise and breast cancer risk for hormone-positive or hormone-negative cancers.[<a href="#cit/section_9.10">

256910</a>

2570] Two meta-analyses yielded the same conclusions. One meta-analysis included 38 prospective trials performed from 1987 to 2014. The summary RR was 0.88 (95% CI, 0.85&#8211;0.90); results were similar for hormone-positive or hormone-negative cancers.[<a href="#cit/section_9.11">

257111</a>

2572] Another meta-analysis reviewed 139 studies encompassing 236,955 women with 3,963 controls. Women who exercised had a significant decrease in breast cancer (odds ratio, 0.78; 95% CI, 0.76&#8211;0.81), with a similar effect size for premenopausal and postmenopausal women.[<a href="#cit/section_9.12">

257312</a>

2574]</p>

2575</section>

N Engl J Med 331 (1): 5-9, 1994.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=8202106&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=8202106&amp;dopt=Abstract">

2590</li>

2593<li id="section_9.5">

2594Gail MH, Brinton LA, Byar DP, et al.: Projecting individualized probabilities of developing breast cancer for white females who are being examined annually. J Natl Cancer Inst 81 (24): 1879-86, 1989.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=2593165&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=2593165&amp;dopt=Abstract">

2635Interventions With Adequate Evidence of Benefit</h2>

2636

2637 <div id="_section_432" class="pdq-sections">

2638<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

2639<h6>

2640In This Section</h6>

2641<ul>

2642<li>

2644Selective Estrogen Receptor Modulators (SERMs)</a>

2645</li>

2646<li>

2649</li>

2660<section id="_158">

2661<h3 id="_158_toc">

2662Selective Estrogen Receptor Modulators (SERMs)</h3>

2663<p id="_159" tabindex="-1">

2664Tamoxifen is used to treat metastatic breast cancer and to suppress local recurrences and new primary breast cancers after surgical excision of breast cancer.[<a href="#cit/section_10.1">

26651</a>

2666] Tamoxifen also maintains bone density among postmenopausal women with breast cancer.[<a href="#cit/section_10.2">

26672</a>

2668-<a href="#cit/section_10.6">

26696</a>

2670] Adverse effects include hot flashes, venous thromboembolic events, and endometrial cancer.[<a href="#cit/section_10.7">

26717</a>

2672-<a href="#cit/section_10.9">

26739</a>

2674] </p>

2675<p id="_160" tabindex="-1">

2676The Breast Cancer Prevention Trial (BCPT) randomly assigned 13,388 patients at elevated risk of breast cancer to receive tamoxifen or placebo.[<a href="#cit/section_10.10">

267710</a>

2678,<a href="#cit/section_10.11">

267911</a>

2680]

2681 The study was closed early because the

2682incidence of breast cancer for the tamoxifen group was 49% lower than for the control group (85 vs. 154 invasive breast cancer cases and 31 vs. 59 <em>

2683in situ</em>

2684 cases at 4 years). Tamoxifen-treated women also had fewer fractures (47 vs. 71) but more endometrial cancer (33 vs. 14 cases) and thrombotic events (99 vs. 70), including pulmonary emboli (17 vs. 6).[<a href="#cit/section_10.11">

268511</a>

2686] </p>

2687<p id="_299" tabindex="-1">

2688An update of the BCPT results after 7 years of follow-up confirmed and extended those results.[<a href="#cit/section_10.12">

268912</a>

2690] Benefits and risks of tamoxifen were not significantly different from those in the original report, with persistent benefit of fewer fractures and persistent increased risk of endometrial cancer, thrombosis, and cataract surgery. No overall mortality benefit was observed after 7 years of follow-up (relative risk [RR], 1.10; 95% confidence interval [CI], 0.85&#8211;1.43).</p>

2691<p id="_631" tabindex="-1">

2692Three other trials of tamoxifen for primary prevention of breast cancer have been completed.[<a href="#cit/section_10.13">

269313</a>

2694-<a href="#cit/section_10.15">

269515</a>

2696]</p>

2697<div class="pdq-content-list">

2698<ul id="_671">

2699<li>

2700A study in the United Kingdom [<a href="#cit/section_10.13">

270113</a>

2702]

2703enrolled 2,471 women at increased breast cancer risk because of a family

2704history of breast and/or ovarian cancer. Risk of estrogen receptor&#8211;positive (ER-positive) breast cancer was significantly reduced in the tamoxifen arm (hazard ratio [HR], 0.61; 95% CI, 0.43&#8211;0.86), an effect noted predominantly in the posttreatment period. Overall, tamoxifen was associated with decreased breast cancer risk at 13 years (HR, 0.78; 95% CI, 0.58&#8211;1.04) but not at 6 years (RR, 1.06).[<a href="#cit/section_10.16">

270516</a>

2706]</li>

2707<li>

2708An Italian study [<a href="#cit/section_10.14">

270914</a>

2710] focused on 5,408

2711women who had undergone hysterectomy and who were described as low to normal

2712risk. After nearly 4 years of follow-up, no protective effect of tamoxifen was observed. Longer follow-up and subgroup analysis in this trial found a protective effect of tamoxifen among women at high risk for hormone receptor&#8211;positive breast cancer (RR, 0.24; 95% CI, 0.10&#8211;0.59) and among women who were taking HT during the trial (RR, 0.43; 95% CI, 0.20&#8211;0.95).[<a href="#cit/section_10.17">

271317</a>

2714,<a href="#cit/section_10.18">

271518</a>

2716]</li>

2717<li>

2718The International Breast Cancer Intervention Study (IBIS-I) randomly assigned 7,152 women aged 35 to 70 years who were at an increased risk of breast cancer to receive tamoxifen (20 mg/day) or placebo for 5 years.[<a href="#cit/section_10.15">

271915</a>

2720] After a median follow-up of 50 months, fewer tamoxifen-treated women had developed invasive or <em>

2721in situ</em>

2722 breast cancer (absolute rate, 4.6 vs. 6.75 per 1,000 woman-years; risk reduction, 32%; 95% CI, 8%&#8211;50%). The RR reduction in ER-positive invasive breast cancer was 31%; there was no reduction in estrogen receptor&#8211;negative (ER-negative) cancers. The nonsignificant increase in all-cause mortality in the tamoxifen group (25 vs. 11; <em>

2723P</em>

2724 = .028) was attributed to chance. The beneficial effect of tamoxifen on breast cancer persisted beyond active treatment; 46 months after the 5-year treatment, fewer women in the tamoxifen arm developed breast cancer (142 vs. 195 cases; RR, 0.73; 95% CI, 0.58&#8211;0.91).[<a href="#cit/section_10.19">

272519</a>

2726] </li>

2727</ul>

2728</div>

2729<p id="_231" tabindex="-1">

2730A meta-analysis of these primary prevention tamoxifen trials showed a 38% reduction in the incidence of breast cancer without statistically significant heterogeneity.[<a href="#cit/section_10.9">

27319</a>

2732] ER-positive tumors were reduced by 48%. Rates of endometrial cancer were increased (consensus RR, 2.4; 95% CI, 1.5&#8211;4.0), as were venous thromboembolic events (RR, 1.9; 95% CI, 1.4&#8211;2.6). None of these primary prevention trials was designed to detect differences in breast cancer mortality. </p>

2733<p id="_163" tabindex="-1">

2734Women with a history of ductal carcinoma <em>

2735in situ</em>

2736 (DCIS) are at increased risk

2737 for contralateral breast cancer. The National Surgical Adjuvant Breast and Bowel Project (NSABP) trial B-24 addressed their management. Women were randomly assigned to receive lumpectomy and radiation therapy either with or without adjuvant tamoxifen. At 6 years, the tamoxifen-treated women had fewer invasive and <em>

2738in situ</em>

2739 breast cancers (8.2% vs. 13.4%; RR, 0.63; 95% CI, 0.47&#8211;0.83). The risk of contralateral breast cancer was also lower in women treated with tamoxifen (RR, 0.49; 95% CI, 0.26&#8211;0.87).[<a href="#cit/section_10.20">

274020</a>

2741]</p>

2742<p id="_165" tabindex="-1">

2743Raloxifene hydrochloride (Evista) is a SERM that has antiestrogenic effects on breast

2744 and estrogenic effects on bone, lipid metabolism, and

2745blood clotting. Unlike tamoxifen, it has antiestrogenic effects on the endometrium.[<a href="#cit/section_10.21">

274621</a>

2747] The Multiple Outcomes of Raloxifene Evaluation (MORE) trial was a

2748randomized, double-blind trial that evaluated 7,705 postmenopausal women with

2749osteoporosis from 1994 to 1998 at 180 clinical centers in the United States. Vertebral fractures were reduced. The

2750effect on breast cancer incidence was a secondary endpoint. After a median follow-up of 47 months, the risk of invasive breast cancer was

2751decreased in the raloxifene-treated women (RR, 0.25; 95% CI, 0.17&#8211;0.45).[<a href="#cit/section_10.22">

275222</a>

2753] As with tamoxifen, raloxifene

2754 reduced the risk of ER-positive breast cancer but not

2755ER-negative breast cancer and was associated with an excess risk of hot flashes and thromboembolic events. No excess risk of endometrial cancer or hyperplasia was

2756observed.[<a href="#cit/section_10.23">

275723</a>

2758]</p>

2759<p id="_253" tabindex="-1">

2760An extension of the MORE trial was the Continuing Outcomes Relevant to Evista (CORE) trial, which studied about 80% of MORE participants in their randomly assigned groups for an additional 4 years. Although there was a median 10-month gap between the two studies, and only about 55% of women were adherent to their assigned medications, the raloxifene group continued to experience a lower incidence of invasive ER-positive breast cancer. The overall reduction in invasive breast cancer during the 8 years of MORE and CORE was 66% (HR, 0.34; 95% CI, 0.22&#8211;0.50); the reduction for ER-positive invasive breast cancer was 76% (HR, 0.24; 95% CI, 0.15&#8211;0.40).[<a href="#cit/section_10.24">

276124</a>

2762]</p>

2763<p id="_466" tabindex="-1">

2764The Raloxifene Use for the Heart trial was a randomized, placebo-controlled trial to evaluate the effects of raloxifene on incidence of coronary events and invasive breast cancer. As in the MORE and CORE studies, raloxifene reduced the risk of invasive breast cancer (HR, 0.56; 95% CI, 0.38&#8211;0.83).[<a href="#cit/section_10.25">

276525</a>

2766]</p>

2767<p id="_328" tabindex="-1">

2768The Study of Tamoxifen and Raloxifene (STAR) (NSABP P-2) compared tamoxifen and raloxifene in 19,747 high-risk women who were monitored for a mean of 3.9 years. Invasive breast cancer incidence was approximately the same for both drugs, but there were fewer noninvasive cancers in the tamoxifen group. Adverse events of uterine cancer, venous thromboembolic events, and cataracts were more common in tamoxifen-treated women, and there was no difference in ischemic heart disease events, strokes, or fractures.[<a href="#cit/section_10.26">

276926</a>

2770] Treatment-associated symptoms of dyspareunia, musculoskeletal problems, and weight gain occurred less frequently in tamoxifen-treated women, whereas vasomotor flushing, bladder control symptoms, gynecologic symptoms, and leg cramps occurred less frequently in those receiving raloxifene.[<a href="#cit/section_10.27">

277127</a>

2772]</p>

2952<section id="_199">

2953<h3 id="_199_toc">

2954Aromatase Inhibitors or Inactivators (Als)</h3>

2955<p id="_200" tabindex="-1">

2956Another class of agents used to treat women with hormone-sensitive breast cancer may also prevent breast cancer. These drugs interfere with aromatase, the adrenal enzyme that allows estrogen production in postmenopausal women. Anastrozole and letrozole inhibit aromatase activity, whereas exemestane inactivates the enzyme. Side effects for all three drugs include fatigue, arthralgia, myalgia, decreased bone mineral density, and increased fracture rate. </p>

2957<p id="_716" tabindex="-1">

2958Women with a previous diagnosis of breast cancer have a lower risk of recurrence and of new breast cancers when treated with AIs, as shown in the following studies:</p>

2959<div class="pdq-content-list">

2960<ol id="_717">

2961<li>

2962In the Arimidex, Tamoxifen, Alone or in Combination trial, which compared anastrozole with tamoxifen as adjuvant therapy for primary breast cancer, the rate of locoregional and distant recurrence was lower for anastrozole when compared with tamoxifen (7.1% vs. 8.5%) but higher for the combination (9.1%).[<a href="#cit/section_10.28">

296328</a>

2964] Anastrozole was also more effective in reducing the incidence of new contralateral breast cancer (0.4% vs. 1.1% vs. 0.9%).</li>

2965<li>

2966In another trial, 5,187 women who received 5 years of adjuvant tamoxifen were randomly assigned to receive either letrozole or placebo.[<a href="#cit/section_10.29">

296729</a>

2968] After only 2.5 years of median follow-up, the study was terminated because previously defined efficacy endpoints had been reached. Patients treated with letrozole had a lower incidence of locoregional and distant cancer recurrence and a lower incidence of new contralateral breast cancer (14 vs. 26).</li>

2969<li>

2970Another placebo-controlled trial of 1,918 women with breast cancer examined the effect of extending letrozole treatment for an additional 5 years in women who had received adjuvant tamoxifen followed by 5 years of letrozole.[<a href="#cit/section_10.30">

297130</a>

2972] At a median of 6.3 years from study entry, the extended letrozole group had an improved 5-year disease-free survival rate of 95% (95% CI, 93%&#8211;96%) compared with 91% (95% CI, 89%&#8211;93%) for the control group (HR, 0.66) but no difference in overall survival. The difference in new contralateral breast cancer diagnoses was statistically significant: 21% (95% CI, 10%&#8211;32%) for the extended letrozole group compared with 49% (32%&#8211;67%) for the control group (HR, 0.42). Women treated with letrozole had an increased risk of bone pain (18% vs. 14%), bone fracture (14% vs. 9%), and new-onset osteoporosis (11% vs. 6%).</li>

2973<li>

2974A trial randomly assigned 4,742 women who had received 2 years of adjuvant tamoxifen to either continue the tamoxifen or switch to exemestane.[<a href="#cit/section_10.31">

297531</a>

2976] After 2.4 years of median follow-up, the exemestane group had a decreased risk of local or metastatic recurrence and a decreased incidence of new contralateral breast cancer (9 vs. 20).</li>

2977</ol>

2978</div>

2979<p id="_718" tabindex="-1">

2980Aromatase inhibitors or inactivators also have been shown to prevent breast cancer in women at increased risk, as shown in the following studies:</p>

2981<div class="pdq-content-list">

2982<ol id="_719">

2983<li>

2984An RCT of primary prevention of breast cancer compared exemestane with placebo in 4,560 women with at least one risk factor (age &gt;60 years, a Gail 5-year risk &gt;1.66%, or a history of DCIS with mastectomy). After 35 months of median follow-up, invasive breast cancer was diagnosed less frequently in the exemestane group (11 vs. 32; HR, 0.35; 95% CI, 0.18&#8211;0.70; number needed-to-treat, about 100 for 35 months). Compared with the placebo group, the exemestane-treated women had more hot flashes (increase, 8%) and fatigue (increase, 2%) but no difference in fractures or cardiovascular events.[<a href="#cit/section_10.32">

298532</a>

2986]</li>

2987<li>

2988The International Breast Cancer Intervention Study II (IBIS-II) randomly assigned 3,864 postmenopausal women who were at increased risk of developing breast cancer to receive either daily anastrozole (1 mg) or placebo for 5 years.[<a href="#cit/section_10.33">

298933</a>

2990] After a median follow-up of 5 years, fewer breast cancers (invasive and DCIS) occurred in the anastrozole-treated group than in the placebo group (HR, 0.47; 95% CI, 0.32&#8211;0.68). The risk of hormone receptor&#8211;positive, but not hormone receptor&#8211;negative, breast cancer was reduced. Additional follow-up, up to a median of 131 months, showed continued benefit for women treated with anastrozole, who had a 49% reduction in breast cancer (HR, 0.51; 95% CI, 0.39&#8211;0.66). No difference in breast cancer mortality was observed.[<a href="#cit/section_10.34">

299134</a>

2992] Women treated with anastrozole were more likely than those taking placebo to have musculoskeletal symptoms, including arthralgias (51% vs. 46%), joint stiffness (7% vs. 5%), carpal tunnel syndrome (3% vs. 2%); hypertension (5% vs. 3%); vasomotor symptoms (57% vs. 49%); and dry eyes (4% vs. 2%).</li>

2993</ol>

2994</div>

2995</section>

2996<section id="_166">

2997<h3 id="_166_toc">

2998Prophylactic Mastectomy</h3>

2999<p id="_167" tabindex="-1">

3000A retrospective cohort study evaluated the impact of bilateral prophylactic mastectomy on breast cancer incidence among women at high and moderate risk on the basis of family history.[<a href="#cit/section_10.35">

300135</a>

3002] <em class="gene-name">

3003BRCA</em>

3004 mutation status was not known. Subcutaneous, rather than total, mastectomy was performed in 90% of these women. After a median follow-up of 14 years postsurgery, the risk reduction for the 425 moderate-risk women was 89%; for the 214 high-risk women, it was 90% to 94%, depending on the method used to calculate expected rates of breast cancer. The risk reduction for breast cancer mortality was 100% for moderate-risk women and 81% for high-risk women. Because the study used family history as a risk indicator rather than genetic testing, breast cancer risk may be overestimated.</p>

3005<p id="_689" tabindex="-1">

3006Contralateral prophylactic mastectomy (CPM) refers to the surgical removal of the opposite uninvolved breast in women who present with unilateral breast cancer. Women who undergo CPM therefore generally undergo bilateral mastectomy for the treatment of unilateral breast cancer, and rates of this procedure among women with unilateral disease (DCIS and early-stage invasive breast cancer) was reported to have increased from 1.9% in 1998 to 11.2% in 2011 based on data from the U.S. National Cancer Data Base.[<a href="#cit/section_10.36">

300736</a>

3008]</p>

3009<p id="_784" tabindex="-1">

3010Some observational studies have suggested that CPM is associated with reduced breast cancer mortality, but these results are generally attributed to selection bias. As of yet, there is no high-quality evidence that CPM is associated with improvements in overall survival. However, some women with unilateral breast cancer, who have a high risk of developing contralateral breast cancer, may reasonably choose CPM to reduce the risk of a new primary cancer in the opposite breast.[<a href="#cit/section_10.37">

301137</a>

3012]</p>

3013</section>

3014<section id="_193">

3015<h3 id="_193_toc">

3016Prophylactic Oophorectomy</h3>

3017<p id="_194" tabindex="-1">

3018Ovarian ablation and oophorectomy are associated with decreased breast cancer risk in average-risk women and in women with increased risk resulting from thoracic irradiation. For more information, see the <a href="/types/breast/hp/breast-prevention-pdq#_37">

3019Endogenous Estrogen</a>

3020 section. Observational studies of women with high breast cancer risk resulting from <em class="gene-name">

3021BRCA1</em>

3022 or <em class="gene-name">

3023BRCA2</em>

3024 gene mutations showed that prophylactic oophorectomy to prevent ovarian cancer was also associated with a 50% decrease in breast cancer incidence.[<a href="#cit/section_10.38">

302538</a>

3026-<a href="#cit/section_10.40">

302740</a>

3028] These studies are confounded by selection bias, family relationships between patients and controls, indications for oophorectomy, and inadequate information about hormone use. A prospective cohort study had similar findings, with a greater breast cancer risk reduction in <em class="gene-name">

3029BRCA2</em>

3030 mutation carriers than in <em class="gene-name">

3031BRCA1</em>

3032 carriers.[<a href="#cit/section_10.41">

303341</a>

3034]</p>

3035</section>
